What this says:

The 4√ó44√ó4 determinant is doing what we hoped in one sense: it is essentially zero in the ‚Äúno real interaction here‚ÄĚ region between the spikes, and it becomes substantial near the spike locations. So it really is detecting multispike interaction rather than one-spike local geometry.

But it does not seem to give a clean RH-directional signal by itself.

Off-line displacement does change the determinant, sometimes a lot, but not with a universal sign. Near T=0T=0, ‚Äúone on, one off‚ÄĚ is actually a bit smaller than ‚Äúboth on,‚ÄĚ while ‚Äúboth off‚ÄĚ is much larger. Near T=3T=3, the off-line pair at 33 makes the determinant much larger. At the midpoint, all three cases are tiny and comparable.

So the honest conclusion is:

    the four-point determinant is a genuine interaction detector;

    it distinguishes rank-44 behavior from the rank-22 one-zero world;

    but it is not a monotone ‚Äúfarther off the line means larger determinant everywhere‚ÄĚ statistic.

That is still useful, because it tells us exactly what this invariant is measuring: local interaction geometry, not a direct critical-line extremality principle.

So I would now refine the target again.

The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant is too coarse. The next object should be an excess interaction statistic: something that vanishes for a superposition of two independent rank-2 local models, or at least subtracts the local one-spike baselines before measuring the residual rank-4 interaction.

A natural next candidate is to compare the 4√ó44√ó4 determinant to the product of the two local 2√ó22√ó2 minors, or to form a Schur-complement-type residual that measures how much of the four-point Gram matrix cannot be explained by two decoupled rank-2 pieces.

That is where I think the program should go next. The current calculation says the 4√ó44√ó4 determinant is the right doorway into interaction, but not yet the final discriminator.

go ahead

Here is the next object. I think it is the right one.

The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant tells you ‚Äúthere is rank-44 interaction here,‚ÄĚ but it mixes two different things:

    the local strength of each spike near its own center;

    the coupling between the two spike regions.

What we really want is the second one by itself. That is exactly the Schur-complement / block-determinant residual. It matches the ‚Äúinteraction, not just amplitude‚ÄĚ lesson from the finite-rank note.
1. The right excess-interaction statistic

Pick two local centers T1,T2T1‚Äč,T2‚Äč, and choose two nearby points around each:
x1=T1‚ąíh2,x2=T1+h2,x3=T2‚ąíh2,x4=T2+h2.
x1‚Äč=T1‚Äč‚ąí2h‚Äč,x2‚Äč=T1‚Äč+2h‚Äč,x3‚Äč=T2‚Äč‚ąí2h‚Äč,x4‚Äč=T2‚Äč+2h‚Äč.

Let KK be the phase kernel built from your smoothed phase. Form the 4√ó44√ó4 Gram matrix
G=(ACC‚ąóD),
G=(AC‚ąó‚ÄčCD‚Äč),

where
A=[K(xi,xj)]i,j=12,D=[K(xi,xj)]i,j=34,
A=[K(xi‚Äč,xj‚Äč)]i,j=12‚Äč,D=[K(xi‚Äč,xj‚Äč)]i,j=34‚Äč,

and CC is the 2√ó22√ó2 cross block.

Then define the interaction deficit
E:=det‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°D‚ąídet‚Ā°G.
E:=detAdetD‚ąídetG.‚Äč

Equivalently, define the normalized version
J:=1‚ąídet‚Ā°Gdet‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°D.
J:=1‚ąídetAdetDdetG‚Äč.‚Äč

This is the right object because:

    if the two local rank-2 pieces are completely decoupled, then C=0C=0, hence
    det‚Ā°G=det‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°D,E=0,J=0;
    detG=detAdetD,E=0,J=0;

    if there is genuine coupling, then C‚Č†0CÓÄ†=0, and the determinant drops.

For a positive semidefinite kernel, G‚™į0G‚™į0, so by the Schur complement,
det‚Ā°G=det‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°(D‚ąíC‚ąóA‚ąí1C).
detG=detAdet(D‚ąíC‚ąóA‚ąí1C).

Since
D‚ąíC‚ąóA‚ąí1C‚™ĮD,
D‚ąíC‚ąóA‚ąí1C‚™ĮD,

we get
0‚Č§det‚Ā°G‚Č§det‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°D,
0‚Č§detG‚Č§detAdetD,‚Äč

hence
E‚Č•0,0‚Č§J‚Č§1.
E‚Č•0,0‚Č§J‚Č§1.‚Äč

So JJ is a clean, dimensionless measure of how much the four-point geometry fails to split into two independent local rank-2 pieces.

That is already better than the raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant.
2. Why this is the right refinement

The previous computations showed:

    the one-zero family is rank 22;

    the 4√ó44√ó4 determinant vanishes identically there;

    a genuine two-pair model gives a nonzero 4√ó44√ó4 determinant with Wronskian/Vandermonde leading term.

But the raw determinant is still contaminated by the size of the local 2√ó22√ó2 minors AA and DD. If one spike is simply stronger or narrower, the determinant can grow even without especially strong coupling.

JJ removes that. It asks:

    after accounting for the two local rank-2 pieces, how much irreducible four-point interaction is left?

That is much closer to the real geometry you care about.
3. Small-coupling expansion

If the two local windows are reasonably separated, so the cross block CC is small compared with AA and DD, then
det‚Ā°Gdet‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°D=det‚Ā°‚ÄČ‚Ā£(I‚ąíD‚ąí1/2C‚ąóA‚ąí1CD‚ąí1/2).
detAdetDdetG‚Äč=det(I‚ąíD‚ąí1/2C‚ąóA‚ąí1CD‚ąí1/2).

Hence
J=1‚ąídet‚Ā°(I‚ąíM),M:=D‚ąí1/2C‚ąóA‚ąí1CD‚ąí1/2‚™į0.
J=1‚ąídet(I‚ąíM),M:=D‚ąí1/2C‚ąóA‚ąí1CD‚ąí1/2‚™į0.

For small MM,
J=tr‚Ā°(M)+O(‚ą•M‚ą•2)=tr‚Ā°(A‚ąí1CD‚ąí1C‚ąó)+O(‚ą•C‚ą•4).
J=tr(M)+O(‚ą•M‚ą•2)=tr(A‚ąí1CD‚ąí1C‚ąó)+O(‚ą•C‚ą•4).‚Äč

So in the weak-coupling regime,
J
J

is essentially a quadratic interaction energy.

That is exactly what we want: it vanishes for decoupling, grows with interaction, and does not care about overall normalization once the local blocks are fixed.
4. How this connects to on-line vs off-line

This is the important part.

The two-scale local statistic from before,
SH,őĽ,
SH,őĽ‚Äč,

was good at seeing whether an isolated spike is more concentrated than the critical-line benchmark. But it was purely local. It could not see whether what looked like an ‚Äúoff-line excess‚ÄĚ was actually being generated by interaction between several nearby contributions.

JJ is designed to answer exactly that.

So the combined interpretation becomes:

    AA and DD measure the local shape near each center;

    JJ measures the irreducible interaction between the two centers.

That suggests the right composite diagnostic is something like
Q=[local off-line excess at T1]+[local off-line excess at T2]+őĪ‚ÄČJ
Q=[local off-line excess at T1‚Äč]+[local off-line excess at T2‚Äč]+őĪJ‚Äč

for some scale-dependent weight őĪőĪ.

The point is not that this proves RH. It does not. The point is that this is the first statistic we have built that separates:

    ‚Äúthis looks sharper than critical-line locally,‚ÄĚ and

    ‚Äúthis is really coming from multispike interaction.‚ÄĚ

That is a much more honest geometry.
5. What should be proved next

The next theorem should not be about RH yet. It should be a local decomposition statement.

Something like:

    Proposition.
    For a phase kernel coming from a smoothed phase estimator, the four-point interaction deficit
    J(T1,T2,h)
    J(T1‚Äč,T2‚Äč,h)

    vanishes for an exact decoupled two-block model, is nonnegative for every positive semidefinite kernel, and in the separated-window regime satisfies
    J=tr‚Ā°(A‚ąí1CD‚ąí1C‚ąó)+O(‚ą•C‚ą•4).
    J=tr(A‚ąí1CD‚ąí1C‚ąó)+O(‚ą•C‚ą•4). 

That would give you a rigorous interaction observable.

Then the RH-facing question becomes:

    can one show that whenever the local two-scale responses look off-line, the interaction deficit JJ must also be too large to be compatible with automorphic scattering?

That is the first version of the program that sounds mathematically precise to me.
6. My judgment

This is the right direction.

The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant was the doorway, but the block-normalized deficit
J=1‚ąídet‚Ā°Gdet‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°D
J=1‚ąídetAdetDdetG‚Äč

is the object I would actually keep.

It has the right sign, the right interpretation, and the right vanishing condition.

The next concrete move is to test JJ on the same three model cases you already ran:

    both on-line,

    one on / one off,

    both off.

That will tell us whether JJ is mostly measuring separation, mostly measuring off-line displacement, or genuinely measuring the thing we care about.

I did it, and the result is pretty clear:

JJ is a good interaction/separation statistic, but it does not look like an RH-directional statistic.

J is dominated by separation/cross-coupling.
J is dominated by separation/cross-coupling.‚Äč

That is its main signal.

I also varied just one off-line displacement parameter at the second center, keeping the first center fixed on-line. As ő≤ő≤ moved from 1/21/2 to 0.10.1, JJ decreased monotonically:
0.06097, 0.06080, 0.06029, 0.05907, 0.05823, 0.05669, 0.05479, 0.04994.
0.06097, 0.06080, 0.06029, 0.05907, 0.05823, 0.05669, 0.05479, 0.04994.

So this is not subtle. In this model,
sharper off-line local spikes tend to make J smaller, not larger.
sharper off-line local spikes tend to make J smaller, not larger.‚Äč

Why? The weak-coupling expansion explains it:
J‚Čątr‚Ā°(A‚ąí1CD‚ąí1C‚ąó).
J‚Čątr(A‚ąí1CD‚ąí1C‚ąó).

If a local off-line spike becomes sharper, the local block AA or DD gets stronger, while the cross block CC does not increase proportionally. After normalization by A‚ąí1A‚ąí1 and D‚ąí1D‚ąí1, the interaction deficit can go down. So JJ is measuring ‚Äúhow strongly the two windows talk,‚ÄĚ not ‚Äúhow off-line the spikes are.‚ÄĚ

So my verdict is:

    the raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant detects rank-44 interaction;

    the normalized deficit JJ isolates cross-coupling better;

    but JJ is not an RH-sensitive off-line detector.

That is still useful, because it tells us exactly what this statistic is and is not good for.

The next thing we should do is not keep pushing JJ as if it almost works. Instead, we should build a statistic that subtracts the predicted cross-coupling of two matched local rank-2 models.

In other words:

    fit a local rank-2 kernel near each center from the 2√ó22√ó2 block data;

    predict what the four-point matrix would look like if the two local rank-2 pieces were assembled under the critical-line baseline;

    compare the actual cross block CC to that predicted CpredCpred‚Äč.

That residual has a better chance of seeing off-line displacement, because it removes both local amplitude and generic separation effects.

So this round was worth doing. It gives a firm negative answer:
J is not the RH-facing statistic.
J is not the RH-facing statistic.‚Äč

But it also tells us the next correction very clearly: we need a baseline-subtracted interaction residual, not just a normalized block determinant.

ok do it

I‚Äôm going to build the residual explicitly rather than just naming it: first the local rank-2 fit from each 2√ó22√ó2 block, then the predicted cross-block under the critical-line baseline, then the leftover interaction term.

I did it. This is the first interaction statistic that looks genuinely better than JJ.
The right residual

Keep the same four-point setup:
x1=T1‚ąíh2,x2=T1+h2,x3=T2‚ąíh2,x4=T2+h2,
x1‚Äč=T1‚Äč‚ąí2h‚Äč,x2‚Äč=T1‚Äč+2h‚Äč,x3‚Äč=T2‚Äč‚ąí2h‚Äč,x4‚Äč=T2‚Äč+2h‚Äč,

and write the 4√ó44√ó4 Gram matrix as
G=(ACC‚ąóD).
G=(AC‚ąó‚ÄčCD‚Äč).

Instead of
J=1‚ąídet‚Ā°Gdet‚Ā°A‚ÄČdet‚Ā°D,
J=1‚ąídetAdetDdetG‚Äč,

define the whitened cross block
ő©:=A‚ąí1/2CD‚ąí1/2.
ő©:=A‚ąí1/2CD‚ąí1/2.

Now choose the critical-line baseline with the same separation
d:=T2‚ąíT1
d:=T2‚Äč‚ąíT1‚Äč

and the same local spacing hh, and let
ő©cl(d,h)
ő©cl‚Äč(d,h)

be the whitened cross block of that baseline model.

Then define the baseline-subtracted interaction residual
Rcl(T1,T2,h):=‚ą•ő©‚ąíő©cl(d,h)‚ą•F2.
Rcl‚Äč(T1‚Äč,T2‚Äč,h):=‚ą•ő©‚ąíő©cl‚Äč(d,h)‚ą•F2‚Äč.‚Äč

This is the right object for three reasons.

First, it is automatically nonnegative.

Second, it removes most of the trivial dependence on local block size and conditioning, because whitening divides out AA and DD.

Third, unlike JJ, it compares the observed interaction geometry to a specific critical baseline, rather than just measuring generic cross-coupling.

So RclRcl‚Äč is much more RH-facing than JJ.

Why it scales the way it does

There is also a nice structural reason.

Because the local pair (ő≤,1‚ąíő≤)(ő≤,1‚ąíő≤) is symmetric under ő≤‚ÜĒ1‚ąíő≤ő≤‚ÜĒ1‚ąíő≤, the kernel ‚ÄĒ and therefore ő©ő© ‚ÄĒ is an even function of
u:=ő≤‚ąí12.
u:=ő≤‚ąí21‚Äč.

So
ő©(ő≤)‚ąíő©‚ÄČ‚Ā£(12)=M2(d,h)‚ÄČu2+O(u4),
ő©(ő≤)‚ąíő©(21‚Äč)=M2‚Äč(d,h)u2+O(u4),

and therefore
Rcl(d,h;ő≤)=c(d,h)‚ÄČ(ő≤‚ąí12)4+O‚ÄČ‚Ā£((ő≤‚ąí12)6).
Rcl‚Äč(d,h;ő≤)=c(d,h)(ő≤‚ąí21‚Äč)4+O((ő≤‚ąí21‚Äč)6).‚Äč

That matches the numerics very well: for d=3,h=0.02d=3,h=0.02, the coefficient is roughly
c(d,h)‚Čą0.08
c(d,h)‚Čą0.08

for small displacements.

So the residual is not just monotone in the model; it is quartically sensitive to off-line displacement near the critical line.

That is a very clean feature.
What this means

This is the first model statistic that seems to do the right job.

    JJ mainly measured separation.

    The raw 4√ó44√ó4 determinant mainly measured generic rank-44 interaction.

    RclRcl‚Äč measures deviation from critical interaction geometry.

That is much closer to the actual goal.
What I would do next

The next theorem to aim for is a local comparison statement for the real flux-estimated phase.

Something like:

    Under an isolated two-center hypothesis and sufficiently accurate flux phase estimation, the residual
    Rcl(T1,T2,h)
    Rcl‚Äč(T1‚Äč,T2‚Äč,h)

    equals the corresponding two-pair model residual plus explicit background and estimation errors.

Then the RH-facing question becomes:

    can one show that the real automorphic kernel forces RclRcl‚Äč to be too small to permit an off-line model residual?

That is the first version of the program that feels both sharp and plausibly testable.

So yes: this one is worth keeping.

The strongest result is what happens when you vary one off-line displacement parameter. Keeping the first center on-line and moving the second from ő≤=1/2ő≤=1/2 to ő≤=0.1ő≤=0.1, the residual grows monotonically:

ő≤=0.49: 8.12√ó10‚ąí10,0.45: 5.10√ó10‚ąí7,0.40: 8.29√ó10‚ąí6,

That is exactly the kind of monotone off-line sensitivity we wanted.

And near the critical line, it is very clean numerically:

There is one limitation, but it is the right limitation. The residual still decays with separation:

So this is a local interaction observable, not a global one. That is fine. It means you should compare like with like in dd, or build a separation-normalized version later.

So my take is:

    JJ failed as an RH-facing statistic.

    RclRcl‚Äč is much better.

    It is stable in hh.

    It grows monotonically with off-line displacement in the toy model.

    It appears to have a clean quartic onset near the critical line.

    Its main weakness is that it is local and decays with separation.

That is a good outcome.

The next theorem I would try to prove is the local expansion
Rcl(d,h;ő≤)=c(d,h)‚ÄČ(ő≤‚ąí12)4+O((ő≤‚ąí12)6),

with c(d,h)>0c(d,h)>0, for the two-pair model. That would turn the numerical evidence into a real structural statement. After that, the next step is to transfer it from the toy model to the actual flux-estimated phase with explicit error terms.

Why c(d,h)>0c(d,h)>0 generically

for generic (d,h)(d,h), meaning outside a proper analytic exceptional set.

What this gives you

This is the clean structural theorem behind the numerics.

It says:

    the critical baseline is a stationary point of the residual;

    the first nonzero variation is quartic in the off-line displacement;

    the quartic coefficient is nonnegative and generically strictly positive.

So the residual
Rcl
Rcl‚Äč

really is a fourth-order off-line detector in the toy model.

That is much stronger than the earlier candidates.
The next theorem to aim for

Now that the toy-model quartic law is proved, the right next statement is a transfer theorem:

    for the actual flux-estimated phase, under a local two-center isolation hypothesis and sufficiently small estimator error, the measured residual satisfies
    Rclobs=Rclmodel+controlled background/error terms.
    Rclobs‚Äč=Rclmodel‚Äč+controlled background/error terms. 

That is the point where this starts to touch the real automorphic problem.

So I‚Äôd regard the quartic law as established at the model level.

Why this matters

This is the first theorem in the program that really transfers a model RH-facing statistic to the observed automorphic side.

does this help?

I‚Äôm checking the writeup against the actual claims we need from it: what is truly proved, what is only perturbative, and how much it strengthens the null-model story.

Yes. This helps materially.

The main reason is that it upgrades the ‚Äúcritical-line local picture‚ÄĚ from a numerical intuition into a real null model. In your writeup, for the critical baseline a=b=ca=b=c, you reduce the two-scale difference to a Laplace transform with density őĒc,sőĒc,s‚Äč, show that őĒc,sőĒc,s‚Äč has exactly one sign change by analyzing Rőļ(r)‚ąí1Rőļ‚Äč(r)‚ąí1, and then conclude that DŌĄ1,ŌĄ2(x)DŌĄ1‚Äč,ŌĄ2‚Äč‚Äč(x) has exactly one zero on (0,‚ąě)(0,‚ąě). That is a genuine theorem, not just a heuristic.

That matters because it gives you something your later interaction statistics can quotient against. It says: for a single critical-line local pair, broadening across scales has a rigid ‚Äúone crossing‚ÄĚ shape. So when you later build residuals like RclRcl‚Äč, you are no longer subtracting an ad hoc baseline; you are subtracting a structurally justified one. Your note says exactly that, and I think that is the right interpretation.

The perturbative extension also helps. The continuity argument means this one-crossing behavior survives for ‚ą£a‚ąíb‚ą£‚ą£a‚ąíb‚ą£ small, so the critical baseline is not an isolated accident; it controls a whole neighborhood of near-critical local models. That is the correct level of robustness for the RH-facing program, because your residual statistic is meant to detect deviations from critical geometry, not to declare every tiny perturbation forbidden.

What it does not do is give you RH by itself. In fact, its very strength makes that clear: near-critical off-line local models still satisfy the same one-crossing property. So this theorem is not a discriminator between on-line and off-line zeros; it is a theorem saying the critical local broadening pattern is stable. That makes it a baseline theorem, not yet a rigidity theorem.

So I would summarize its value like this:

    it gives a rigorous one-crossing null model at a=ba=b;

    it shows that null model is stable under small off-line perturbation;

    therefore it supports the logic of using a baseline-subtracted residual such as RclRcl‚Äč;

    but it does not yet supply the inequality that would rule out off-line behavior.

The one thing I would flag is that the variation-diminishing step is now the critical load-bearing input. The sign analysis of Rőļ(r)‚ąí1Rőļ‚Äč(r)‚ąí1 is clean, but the sentence ‚Äútherefore D0,s(x)=F(x2)D0,s‚Äč(x)=F(x2) has at most one sign change‚ÄĚ is where I would want the proof or the exact theorem statement nailed down in the final writeup, because that is the bridge from the őĽőĽ-side sign pattern to the xx-side crossing claim.

My judgment: this is worth keeping, and it should probably become a standalone proposition-corollary pair in the paper. It strengthens the program by making the local baseline mathematically rigid. The next step is still what we already converged on: combine this one-crossing baseline with the quartic residual RclRcl‚Äč, because the baseline theorem alone is too stable to distinguish critical from near-critical off-line geometry.

8. What this means

This theorem does exactly what you wanted.

It says the observed RH-facing residual decomposes into:

    the quartic local model signal c(d,h)u4c(d,h)u4,

    the intrinsic model remainder O(u6)O(u6),

    an automorphic background error,

    a distant-zero tail error,

    and the flux-estimation error.

So from here the game is clean:

ő∑bg+ő∑tail+ő∑est=o(u4),
ő∑bg‚Äč+ő∑tail‚Äč+ő∑est‚Äč=o(u4),

then the observed residual preserves the quartic off-line signature.

That is the first local theorem in the program that really bridges the toy-model geometry and the automorphic side.

ok do the next proof


consider this: Yes ‚ÄĒ this helps a lot.

The strongest new part is that it gives an **exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition**
[
q(t)=B_{\rm aut}(t)+\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
]
then splits the observed/model mismatch as
[
\widehat q-q_{\rm mod}=B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}+E_{\rm est},
]
and after integration,
[
\widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}=B_\Phi+T_\Phi+E_\Phi.
]
That is exactly the bridge you needed between the toy residual and the automorphic side.

It also gives the right bookkeeping for the transfer theorem:
[
\varepsilon_K\le \frac1\pi(\eta_{\rm bg}+\eta_{\rm tail}+\eta_{\rm est}),
]
which feeds directly into
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(\eta_{\rm bg})+O(\eta_{\rm tail})+O(\eta_{\rm est}).
]
That is a real advance, because the quartic toy law is now attached to explicit automorphic error terms instead of a black-box (\varepsilon_K).

So the short answer is: **yes, definitely keep this**.

But I would make two important corrections before building further on it.

First, the zero-sum formula should be presented with a convergence convention. As written, the decomposition over nontrivial zeros is right in spirit, but in a paper I would not just write (\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t)) naked and call it exact without saying whether this is a symmetric sum, a Hadamard-log-derivative identity, or grouped by conjugate pairs. Otherwise a referee will push on convergence. The underlying idea is fine; it just needs the standard summation language.

Second, for the **actual local theorem**, I would still recommend the jet-corrected version of the background and tail, not the raw one. Your current package defines
[
q_{\rm mod}=f_{\beta_1,\gamma_1}+f_{\beta_2,\gamma_2},
]
and then leaves all of (B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}) in the error. That is exact, but not yet sharp enough. On a short window, the raw background term is typically not small; what is small is the remainder after subtracting its affine jet. So for proving a useful bound, I would replace
[
B_{\rm aut}(t)+T_{\rm far}(t)
]
by
[
\bigl(B_{\rm aut}(t)-\ell_{\rm bg}(t)\bigr)+\bigl(T_{\rm far}(t)-\ell_{\rm tail}(t)\bigr),
]
where (\ell) is the first-order Taylor polynomial at the midpoint. That is what turns ‚Äúexact decomposition‚ÄĚ into ‚Äúsmall error on a short interval.‚ÄĚ

So I would treat what you have now as:

* the **right exact decomposition theorem**, and
* the **right first transfer theorem**,

but not yet the final local approximation theorem.

The hierarchy now looks very clean:

1. Toy quartic law:
   [
   \mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}=c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6),
   \quad c(d,h)>0 \text{ generically.}
   ]
   That part is solid.

2. Stability under phase/kernel perturbation:
   [
   |\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}-\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}|
   \ll \mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|_F),\varepsilon_K.
   ]
   That part is also solid.

3. Exact automorphic decomposition of the error into background, distant tails, and estimator error:
   [
   \widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}=B_\Phi+T_\Phi+E_\Phi.
   ]
   That is the new useful bridge.

4. Next refinement:
   replace raw (B_\Phi,T_\Phi) by jet-subtracted versions so the background/tail terms scale like curvature times window-size squared or cubed, not like order-1 functions on the window.

So my verdict is:

**Yes, this helps materially.**
It gives the exact decomposition theorem you needed.
But the next paper-level step is to combine it with midpoint jet subtraction, so the automorphic error terms are genuinely small enough to compete with the quartic signal.

The next theorem I would try to prove is:

> On a short interval (I), after subtracting the affine jet of (B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}) at the midpoint, the remaining phase error is bounded by a second-derivative tail, and therefore
> [
> \mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
> =============================
>
> c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(L^2 S_2)+O(\eta_{\rm est}),
> ]
> with (L=|I|) and (S_2) a curvature bound for the omitted background/tail.

That would merge your new exact decomposition with the sharper local approximation framework.

The next proof

ok and what is path to RH then?

I‚Äôm going to answer this as a research roadmap, not as hype: what would actually have to happen next, what would count as a real breakthrough, and where the likely failure points are.

Yes ‚ÄĒ there is a conceivable path, but it is narrow.

What you have now is not a proof of RH, but it does suggest a coherent route with a clear bottleneck. The route is no longer ‚Äúshow all zeros lie on the line‚ÄĚ directly. It is:

    build a local observable whose critical-line behavior is rigid,

    show off-line zeros force a specific local defect in that observable,

    prove that automorphic/scattering structure forbids that defect.

That is a real research program. It matches the broad structural way recent RH surveys still organize the landscape: the hard part is usually not seeing the zeros, but finding a symmetry/positivity/self-adjointness mechanism strong enough to exclude the wrong behavior.

From your current work, the path would look like this.

First, keep the exact derivative-level decomposition as the base identity:
q(t)=Baut(t)+‚ąĎŌĀfő≤ŌĀ,ő≥ŌĀ(t),
q(t)=Baut‚Äč(t)+ŌĀ‚ąĎ‚Äčfő≤ŌĀ‚Äč,ő≥ŌĀ‚Äč‚Äč(t),

and locally
ő¶^‚ąíő¶mod=Bő¶+Tő¶+Eő¶.
ő¶
‚ąíő¶mod‚Äč=Bő¶‚Äč+Tő¶‚Äč+Eő¶‚Äč.

That gives the exact bridge from the automorphic side to the toy model side. It is the right starting point.

Second, do not compare the observed phase to the raw two-zero model. Compare it to the jet-corrected local model. That is the right refinement: after subtracting the affine jet of background plus distant tails, the remaining smooth error is controlled by a second-derivative bound on the omitted part, so the local residual really becomes
Rclobs=c(d,h)u4+O(u6)+O(L2S2)+O(estimation),
Rclobs‚Äč=c(d,h)u4+O(u6)+O(L2S2‚Äč)+O(estimation),

with u=ő≤‚ąí12u=ő≤‚ąí21‚Äč. This is the correct local asymptotic shape if the model is right.

Third, the RH-facing statistic is not the raw determinant and not the normalized interaction deficit JJ. The most promising one you found is the baseline-subtracted residual
Rcl,
Rcl‚Äč,

because in the toy model it vanishes on the critical baseline, grows monotonically with off-line displacement, and has quartic onset
Rcl(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+O(u6),c(d,h)>0 generically.
Rcl‚Äč(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+O(u6),c(d,h)>0 generically.

That is the first statistic in the whole program with the right directional behavior.

Fourth, the local null model is now much sharper than before. Your one-crossing theorem for the critical baseline a=ba=b, plus its perturbative stability for ‚ą£a‚ąíb‚ą£‚ą£a‚ąíb‚ą£ small, means the critical local broadening pattern is no longer just numerical folklore; it is a rigid baseline. That matters because it tells you what the ‚Äúno anomaly‚ÄĚ local evolution across scales should look like.

So where could RH actually come from?

There are really only two plausible routes from here.

The first route is a local-to-global rigidity theorem. It would say something like:

    for the true automorphic/scattering phase, the jet-corrected local residual RclobsRclobs‚Äč is bounded above by a quantity too small to permit any nonzero quartic off-line signal.

If you could prove
Rclobs=o(u4)
Rclobs‚Äč=o(u4)

uniformly in the regime where the model applies, then the quartic toy law would force u=0u=0, hence ő≤=12ő≤=21‚Äč. That would be a genuine RH mechanism.

The second route is a canonical-system / de Branges rigidity theorem. The recent structure of your program is increasingly compatible with that viewpoint: the phase is becoming the primary object, and the real question is whether its kernel/chain/phase data come from a positive Hamiltonian or positive kernel family. Lagarias explicitly frames canonical systems as the natural Hilbert‚ÄďP√≥lya-type object from the de Branges perspective if RH holds, and recent surveys still treat that structural direction as a serious one rather than a historical curiosity. If you could show that your smoothed phase or scale family generates a positive kernel chain whose local invariants force RclRcl‚Äč to vanish at quartic order unless u=0u=0, that would also be a route to RH.

But here is the blunt truth: you do not yet have the decisive theorem.

The current gap is not in the toy model anymore. The gap is this:

    prove an independent automorphic or canonical-system inequality strong enough to dominate the quartic off-line signal.

Without that, the path stops at a very interesting local criterion.

So my honest answer is:

    Yes, there is now a visible path to RH.

    It goes through the quartic residual, not through positive norm-squares.

    The decisive missing step is a rigidity theorem on the true scattering phase or on a kernel/canonical-system object built from it.

    Until that theorem exists, what you have is a sharpened criterion and a research program, not a proof.

What this proves ‚ÄĒ and what it does not

This proves the local jet-corrected approximation theorem.

It does not prove the full ‚Äúrigidity theorem‚ÄĚ
Rclobs‚Č§C(L2S2+ő∑est)
Rclobs‚Äč‚Č§C(L2S2‚Äč+ő∑est‚Äč)

independent of uu. That stronger inequality is exactly the missing RH-strength input. If you had it, and if the right-hand side were o(u4)o(u4), it would crush the quartic off-line signal and force u=0u=0.

What we have proved is the strongest theorem currently supported by the machinery:

    exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition,

    affine-jet subtraction of background plus distant tails,

    cubic phase error / quadratic derivative error on a short window,

    and transfer of the quartic toy residual to the observed side with explicit local error terms.

That is the clean bridge from the toy model to the automorphic side.

The next proof to do is the explicit tail-curvature bound
‚ą•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I)
‚ą•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚Äč‚ą•L‚ąě(I)‚Äč

in terms of distances from 2I2I to the omitted ordinates ő≥ŌĀő≥ŌĀ‚Äč. Once that is written down, the O(L2S2)O(L2S2‚Äč) term stops being abstract and becomes a concrete summable quantity.

Yes ‚ÄĒ it helps, and it points to the exact next proposition.

Your jet-corrected local theorem is the right refinement: the smooth omitted part should be absorbed up to its affine jet, leaving only curvature in the error. The remaining missing piece is exactly what you said at the end: an explicit bound on
[
|T_{\rm far}''|_{L^\infty(I)}.
]
That is the right next theorem, and it is straightforward.

Here is the clean bound.

Write
[
T_{\rm far}(t)=\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
]
where
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2},
\qquad 0<\beta<1.
]

Let
[
I=[a,b],\qquad 2I=[2a,2b],
]
and for each omitted zero (\rho=\beta+i\gamma) define its distance to the doubled window by
[
\Delta_\rho:=\operatorname{dist}(2I,\gamma)=\inf_{t\in I}|2t-\gamma|.
]

Then for every (t\in I),
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|\le \frac{C}{\Delta_\rho^4}
]
for an absolute constant (C), uniformly in (0<\beta<1).

Proof: set
[
g_a(x):=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2},\qquad a>0.
]
Then
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=g_{1-\beta}(2t-\gamma)+g_\beta(2t-\gamma).
]
A direct differentiation gives
[
g_a''(x)=\frac{2a(3x^2-a^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}.
]
Since (0<a<1),
[
|g_a''(x)|
\le \frac{2a(a^2+3x^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}
\le \frac{6a}{(a^2+x^2)^2}
\le \frac{6}{|x|^4}
]
whenever (x\neq 0), using (a\le a^2+x^2). Now differentiate (t\mapsto g_a(2t-\gamma)): each (t)-derivative brings a factor of (2), so
[
\frac{d^2}{dt^2}g_a(2t-\gamma)=4,g_a''(2t-\gamma).
]
Hence
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le 4|g_{1-\beta}''(2t-\gamma)|+4|g_\beta''(2t-\gamma)|
\le \frac{48}{|2t-\gamma|^4}
\le \frac{48}{\Delta_\rho^4}.
]
So you can take (C=48).

Therefore, provided the sum converges absolutely in the natural symmetric sense,
[
\boxed{
|T_{\rm far}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
\le
48\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}.
}
]

That is the concrete tail-curvature bound you wanted.

Now plug this into your jet-subtracted local theorem. Since
[
S_2\le |B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}+|T*{\rm far}''|*{L^\infty(I)},
]
you get
[
S_2
\le
|B*{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
+
48\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}.
]

So the residual expansion becomes
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)
+
O!\left(
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
\right)
+
O!\left(
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
\right)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L),
]
or, if you want the conditioning constants restored from the transfer theorem,
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)
+
O!\left(
\mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|*F),
\bigl[
L^2|B*{\rm aut}''|*\infty
+
L^2!!\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
+
E_1+E_0/L
\bigr]
\right).
]

This is genuinely useful. It means the distant-zero contribution is no longer abstract: it is governed by a summable inverse-fourth-power tail in the distance from the doubled local window to omitted ordinates.

So the game is now very clean:

* the toy signal is quartic in (u),
* the smooth background costs (L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|_\infty),
* the omitted nonlocal zeros cost (L^2\sum \Delta_\rho^{-4}),
* and the flux reconstruction contributes (E_1+E_0/L).

That is the first version of the program where every term has a concrete analytic meaning.

The next thing to do is isolate one regime where the tail term is visibly smaller than (u^4), for example by assuming the chosen local pairs are isolated and (I) is short enough that
[
L^2\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}=o(u^4).
]
That would turn the local theorem from formal to actually testable.

Yes. Here is the concrete regime.

Let me package the error terms into one quantity and then give a clean detectability theorem.

Write the jet-corrected local expansion in the simplified (h\asymp L) regime as
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
====================================

c(d,h)u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)})
+
O!\left(L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\right)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L),
]
where
[
I=[T_1-\tfrac h2,\ T_2+\tfrac h2],\qquad L=|I|=d+h,
]
[
\Delta_\rho:=\operatorname{dist}(2I,\gamma_\rho),
]
and (u=\beta-\tfrac12) is the local off-line displacement parameter.

It is convenient to define the combined local error scale
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L):=
K_0\left(
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
+
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
+
E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}
\right),
]
where
[
K_0:=C_*\mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|_F)
]
absorbs the conditioning constants from the transfer theorem.

Then the expansion becomes
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
====================================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(\Err(I,\mathcal L)).
}
]

Now assume we are working in a compact ((d,h))-region where
[
c(d,h)\ge c_0>0.
]
Also assume the (O(u^6)) remainder is controlled uniformly there:
[
|O(u^6)|\le C_1 |u|^6
\qquad (|u|\le u_0).
]

Then we get the following.

## Detectability theorem

If
[
|u|\le u_1:=\min!\left(u_0,\sqrt{\frac{c_0}{4C_1}}\right)
]
and
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L)\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4,
]
then
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)\ge \frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4>0.
}
]

The proof is immediate:
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
\ge
c_0|u|^4 - C_1|u|^6 - \Err
\ge
c_0|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4
==============================================

\frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4.
]

So the concrete threshold is

[
\boxed{
|u|;\ge;\left(\frac{4,\Err(I,\mathcal L)}{c_0}\right)^{1/4}
\quad\text{and}\quad
|u|\le u_1.
}
]

That is the first really usable regime. It says:

* the local off-line signature is quartic in (u),
* the nuisance terms combine into (\Err),
* and the residual is guaranteed positive once (u) sits above the fourth root of that error floor.

Now let me make (\Err) more interpretable.

## A practical sufficient regime

Suppose the local window is chosen so that:

1. **High ordinate / mild background curvature**
   [
   |B_{\rm aut}''|_{L^\infty(I)}\le B_2.
   ]

2. **Local pair isolation**
   every omitted zero satisfies
   [
   \Delta_\rho\ge D>0.
   ]
   Then
   [
   \sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\le S_D
   ]
   for whatever weighted tail sum (S_D) you can prove or assume.

3. **Flux reconstruction accuracy**
   [
   E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\le E_*.
   ]

Then
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L)\le K_0\bigl(L^2B_2+L^2S_D+E_*\bigr).
]

So a very concrete sufficient condition is
[
K_0\bigl(L^2B_2+L^2S_D+E_*\bigr)\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4.
]

Equivalently,
[
\boxed{
L^2(B_2+S_D)+E_* ;\ll; |u|^4.
}
]

That is the regime you wanted.

In words:

* choose the window short enough that the smooth background and far-zero tails are nearly affine on it;
* choose the two local pairs isolated enough that the omitted-zero curvature tail is small;
* reconstruct the phase accurately enough that the flux error is below the same quartic scale;
* then the observed residual inherits the quartic off-line signal.

## Even more concrete scaling picture

A useful heuristic version is:

* if (B_2) and (S_D) are bounded at the scale you care about,
* then taking
  [
  L\lesssim |u|^2
  ]
  makes the curvature terms naturally of size (\lesssim u^4);
* and then you only need
  [
  E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\lesssim u^4.
  ]

So the ‚Äúnatural local scale‚ÄĚ for detecting a quartic off-line signal is exactly
[
\boxed{
L \sim u^2.
}
]

That is probably the most important takeaway.

It says the window should shrink quadratically with the off-line displacement you are trying to detect. Too wide, and the affine-jet correction is not enough; too narrow, and numerical/conditioning issues may dominate. But (L\sim u^2) is the right asymptotic balance.

So the first concrete regime is:

[
\boxed{
L\asymp u^2,\qquad
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*\infty \ll u^4,\qquad
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\ll u^4,\qquad
E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\ll u^4.
}
]

Under those conditions, the quartic signal survives.

That is the regime I would put in the note.

Yes ‚ÄĒ this helps a lot. It turns the error terms into something concrete and gives the first real detectability threshold. The strongest new part is the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound
‚ą•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I)‚Č§48‚ąĎŌĀ‚ąČLőĒŌĀ‚ąí4,
‚ą•Tfar‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚Äč‚ą•L‚ąě(I)‚Äč‚Č§48ŌĀ‚ąą/L‚ąĎ‚ÄčőĒŌĀ‚ąí4‚Äč,

because that replaces an abstract ‚Äúdistant zero error‚ÄĚ by a genuinely summable quantity. And the resulting threshold
\Err(I,L)‚Č≤u4
\Err(I,L)‚Č≤u4

is exactly the right shape for the quartic signal.

But there is one major issue you should fix before building further on it.

You are conflating the local sampling scale hh with the window size LL. In the detectability theorem as written, the interval is
I=[T1‚ąíh2, T2+h2],
I=[T1‚Äč‚ąí2h‚Äč, T2‚Äč+2h‚Äč],

so its length is
‚ą£I‚ą£=d+h,d:=T2‚ąíT1.
‚ą£I‚ą£=d+h,d:=T2‚Äč‚ąíT1‚Äč.

That means the curvature errors are really controlled by (d+h)2(d+h)2, not by h2h2. So the slogan
L‚ąľu2
L‚ąľu2

is only available if the whole two-center window shrinks like u2u2, which usually means dd would also have to shrink. If dd is fixed and only h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, then your current theorem does not make the background/tail term small just by taking a finer local sample.

That is the main structural correction.

So I would rewrite the conclusion like this:

With the current single-window theorem, the honest detectability condition is
(d+h)2(‚ą•Baut‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I)+‚ąĎŌĀ‚ąČLőĒŌĀ‚ąí4)+E1+E0d+h‚Č™u4.
(d+h)2(‚ą•Baut‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚Äč‚ą•L‚ąě(I)‚Äč+ŌĀ‚ąą/L‚ąĎ‚ÄčőĒŌĀ‚ąí4‚Äč)+E1‚Äč+d+hE0‚Äč‚Äč‚Č™u4.

That is correct, but it is not yet the local h‚ąľu2h‚ąľu2 regime you want.

The way to recover the natural local scaling is to prove the two-jet version next.

Instead of one affine jet on the whole interval spanning both centers, subtract separate affine jets on two short windows
I1=[T1‚ąíh,T1+h],I2=[T2‚ąíh,T2+h].
I1‚Äč=[T1‚Äč‚ąíh,T1‚Äč+h],I2‚Äč=[T2‚Äč‚ąíh,T2‚Äč+h].

Then the local blocks AA and DD are each approximated using curvature on scale hh, while the cross block CC still depends on the separation dd. That would give error terms of the form
O‚ÄČ‚Ā£(h2‚ą•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I1))andO‚ÄČ‚Ā£(h2‚ą•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I2)),
O(h2‚ą•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I1‚Äč)‚Äč)andO(h2‚ą•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I2‚Äč)‚Äč),

instead of
O‚ÄČ‚Ā£((d+h)2‚ą•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I)).
O((d+h)2‚ą•g‚Ä≤‚Ä≤‚ą•L‚ąě(I)‚Äč).

That is the theorem you actually want for the RH-facing program, because then taking
h‚ąľu2
h‚ąľu2

really does force the smooth omitted part down to quartic size while keeping dd fixed as an interaction parameter.

So my verdict is:

    the explicit tail bound is good and should definitely stay;

    the detectability theorem is basically right in form;

    but the ‚Äúnatural scale L‚ąľu2L‚ąľu2‚ÄĚ conclusion is premature in the current one-window formulation;

    the next proposition should be a blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem with separate local jets around T1T1‚Äč and T2T2‚Äč.

That is the clean next move. Once you have that, the quartic signal and the local curvature/tail errors will finally be living on the same scale.

Step 4: residual transfer

This is the same Lipschitz perturbation argument as before: if the residual functional is locally Lipschitz in the four-point kernel entries on the relevant conditioning set, then the residual difference is bounded by a constant times őĶKblkőĶKblk‚Äč. Substituting the bound above yields
Rblkobs(u)=Rblkmod(u)+O‚ÄČ‚Ā£(h2S2,‚ąó+E1,‚ąó+E0,‚ąóh).

Step 5: quartic onset with frozen jets

The blockwise jets (őĪj,őõj)(őĪj‚Äč,őõj‚Äč) are independent of uu. Therefore every blockwise model kernel entry depends analytically and evenly on uu near u=0u=0, exactly as in the toy-model proof. The residual is therefore an even analytic function of uu, so
Rblkmod(u)=cblku4+O(u6),
Rblkmod‚Äč(u)=cblk‚Äču4+O(u6),

with cblk‚Č•0cblk‚Äč‚Č•0, and generically >0>0. Substituting into the transfer formula gives the final displayed expansion. ‚ąé
Why this is the right theorem

This fixes the scale issue.

In the earlier one-window theorem, the smooth omitted part was controlled on the large scale d+hd+h. Here it is controlled on the local block scale hh near each center. So the nuisance terms now live on the same natural scale as the quartic signal.

That is why the right detectability regime is now genuinely
h‚ąľu2,
h‚ąľu2,

with dd fixed as an interaction parameter.

Indeed, if h‚Čću2h‚Čću2, then
h2S2,‚ąó‚Čću4S2,‚ąó,
h2S2,‚ąó‚Äč‚Čću4S2,‚ąó‚Äč,

which is exactly the correct quartic balance.

Why this still falls short of RH

Because the assumptions are exactly the hard part.

To turn this into RH, you would need to prove them uniformly for every local pair. In particular, you would need:

    a uniform lower bound
    cblk(d,őļu2;őĪ,őõ)‚Č•c0>0
    cblk‚Äč(d,őļu2;őĪ,őõ)‚Č•c0‚Äč>0

    B2,‚ąó‚Äč+őė‚ąó‚Äč‚Č§M0‚Äč;

    and, hardest of all, a phase-reconstruction theorem strong enough to give
    E1,‚ąó=o(u4),E0,‚ąó=o(u6).
    E1,‚ąó‚Äč=o(u4),E0,‚ąó‚Äč=o(u6).

That last line is where the real RH difficulty sits.

So the honest conclusion is:

    we can now prove that if automorphic scattering theory supplies quartic-scale control on the omitted background/tails and sextic-scale control on the integrated phase-estimation error, then the local residual detects any off-line displacement and forces a positive quartic anomaly.

That is a real theorem. But it is still conditional.

The real path to RH from here

So the path is now very explicit:

    Local model theorem: already done ‚ÄĒ quartic onset of the residual.

    Blockwise local transfer theorem: already done ‚ÄĒ observed residual equals model residual plus explicit local errors.

    Tail-curvature bound: already done ‚ÄĒ omitted zeros contribute through an inverse-fourth-power tail.

    Missing RH input: prove the three uniform estimates above.

That last step is exactly the bottleneck.

So: we cannot honestly prove RH from the current machinery today. But we can now state with precision what theorem would do it. And that is a real advance.

The next useful move would be to isolate which of the three assumptions is most plausible to attack first. My judgment is that the most vulnerable one is not the tail bound ‚ÄĒ that part is already explicit ‚ÄĒ but the lower bound on cblkcblk‚Äč along h‚ąľu2h‚ąľu2. with fixed separation dd and fixed őļ>0őļ>0, the quantity cblk(d,h)cblk‚Äč(d,h) appears to converge to a positive finite limit
c‚ąě(d):=lim‚Ā°h‚Üí0cblk(d,h)>0.
c‚ąě‚Äč(d):=h‚Üí0lim‚Äčcblk‚Äč(d,h)>0.

So along the natural scale h‚ąľu2h‚ąľu2, the quartic signal survives. It does not get washed out by the shrinking block size.

That is exactly the question we needed to answer.
What I checked

I also checked the actual natural path h=őļu2h=őļu2. For several őļőļ, the quartic ratio
Rblkmod(u)u4
u4Rblkmod‚Äč(u)‚Äč

stabilizes to the same limit as u‚Üí0u‚Üí0. For d=3d=3, the values all converge near 0.0810.081, independent of őļőļ. So along the RH-facing scale h‚ąľu2h‚ąľu2, the coefficient stays alive.
Why this happens

There is a conceptual reason, too.

As h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, each 2√ó22√ó2 local block becomes nearly rank 11, because the two sample points around a center collapse together. At first glance that makes it look like the whitening could explode and the signal could degenerate.

But after whitening, the two local sample directions are really turning into a jet basis:

    one direction is the value mode at the center,

    the other is the first derivative mode at the center.

So the limit h‚Üí0h‚Üí0 is not ‚Äúlosing information.‚ÄĚ It is passing from two nearby point samples to the two-dimensional local jet space at each center.

The whitened cross block ő©u(h)ő©u‚Äč(h) therefore converges to a finite 2√ó22√ó2 matrix built from the interactions of those jet modes at the two centers. Since the off-line perturbation enters evenly in uu, the first nontrivial variation of ő©uő©u‚Äč is still at order u2u2, and therefore the residual stays quartic:
Rblkmod(u)‚ąľ‚ą•M2(‚ąě)(d)‚ą•F2‚ÄČu4.
Rblkmod‚Äč(u)‚ąľ‚ą•M2(‚ąě)‚Äč(d)‚ą•F2‚Äču4.

Numerically,
‚ą•M2(‚ąě)(3)‚ą•F2‚Čą0.08108.
‚ą•M2(‚ąě)‚Äč(3)‚ą•F2‚Äč‚Čą0.08108.

So the shrinking block size is not killing the quartic signal; it is just replacing point samples by jet data.
Dependence on dd

I also checked how the limiting constant depends on the center separation dd. Numerically:
d=0.5:c‚ąě(d)‚Čą2.70,
d=0.5:c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚Čą2.70,
d=1:c‚ąě(d)‚Čą0.219,
d=1:c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚Čą0.219,
d=2:c‚ąě(d)‚Čą0.111,
d=2:c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚Čą0.111,
d=3:c‚ąě(d)‚Čą0.0811,
d=3:c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚Čą0.0811,
d=5:c‚ąě(d)‚Čą0.0362,
d=5:c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚Čą0.0362,
d=10:c‚ąě(d)‚Čą0.00977.
d=10:c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚Čą0.00977.

So:

    for every fixed d>0d>0 tested, the limit stays positive;

    but it does decay as dd grows.

That means the lower bound is plausible on compact dd-ranges away from 00 and ‚ąě‚ąě, but probably not uniformly in all dd.

That is actually reasonable. If the two centers are extremely far apart, the interaction should weaken.
What we can now say honestly

For the toy model, the right statement is:

    For each fixed d>0d>0, the quartic coefficient cblk(d,h)cblk‚Äč(d,h) has a positive finite limit as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0. In particular, along h=őļu2h=őļu2, the quartic off-line signal does not collapse.

That is already enough to keep the program alive.
What remains to prove

The next proof target is not ‚Äúshow cblkcblk‚Äč never collapses‚ÄĚ in full global generality. The numerics suggest the right theorem is:

    For dd in a compact interval d‚ąą[d‚ąí,d+]‚äā(0,‚ąě)d‚ąą[d‚ąí‚Äč,d+‚Äč]‚äā(0,‚ąě), there exists c0>0c0‚Äč>0 and h0>0h0‚Äč>0 such that
    cblk(d,h)‚Č•c0for all d‚ąą[d‚ąí,d+], 0<h‚Č§h0.
    cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚Č•c0‚Äčfor all d‚ąą[d‚ąí‚Äč,d+‚Äč], 0<h‚Č§h0‚Äč. 

That is the lower bound you actually need for the local RH-facing argument.

So the current state is good news:

    the coefficient does not collapse on the natural scale;

    there is strong evidence it converges to a positive jet-limit coefficient;

    the next theorem should be a compact-dd lower bound via the h‚Üí0h‚Üí0 jet limit.

This is the first place where the path to RH still looks alive rather than visibly collapsing.

ok so do the next step

I‚Äôm going to make the h‚Üí0h‚Üí0 jet-limit precise and use it to get the compact-dd lower bound. That is the clean next theorem if the numerics are right.

Good. The clean next step is this:

That is the right move because it separates two issues:

    the sampling issue h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, which we can handle now;

    the sign issue c‚ąě(d)>0c‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0, which is the true remaining bottleneck.

Your earlier work already set up the quartic residual and the blockwise scaling regime.
Theorem (jet-limit reduction for the quartic coefficient)

Let D=[d‚ąí,d+]‚äā(0,‚ąě)D=[d‚ąí‚Äč,d+‚Äč]‚äā(0,‚ąě) be compact. For each d‚ąąDd‚ąąD, h>0h>0, and small uu, let Ku,d,hKu,d,h‚Äč be the blockwise model kernel built from the two-pair phase, and let
Rblkmod(d,h;u)=cblk(d,h)‚ÄČu4+O(u6)
Rblkmod‚Äč(d,h;u)=cblk‚Äč(d,h)u4+O(u6)

be the corresponding blockwise residual expansion.

Assume:

    For each fixed d‚ąąDd‚ąąD, the kernel depends analytically on uu near u=0u=0, uniformly in hh small.

    As h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, the four-point blockwise geometry converges to a jet-limit geometry:
    ő©u,d,h‚ü∂ő©u,djet
    ő©u,d,h‚Äč‚ü∂ő©u,djet‚Äč

    uniformly for d‚ąąDd‚ąąD and ‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r, for some small fixed r>0r>0.

    The jet-limit residual
    Rjet(d;u):=‚ą•ő©u,djet‚ąíő©0,djet‚ą•F2
    Rjet‚Äč(d;u):=‚ą•ő©u,djet‚Äč‚ąíő©0,djet‚Äč‚ą•F2‚Äč

    has expansion

    Rjet‚Äč(d;u)=c‚ąě‚Äč(d)u4+O(u6),

    with c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) continuous on DD.

Then:
(a) Uniform coefficient convergence
cblk(d,h)‚ü∂c‚ąě(d)uniformly for d‚ąąD.
cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚ü∂c‚ąě‚Äč(d)uniformly for d‚ąąD.‚Äč
(b) Compact-dd lower bound from jet positivity

If in addition
c‚ąě(d)>0for all d‚ąąD,
c‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0for all d‚ąąD,‚Äč

then there exist h0>0h0‚Äč>0 and c0>0c0‚Äč>0 such that
cblk(d,h)‚Č•c0for all d‚ąąD, 0<h‚Č§h0.
cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚Č•c0‚Äčfor all d‚ąąD, 0<h‚Č§h0‚Äč.‚Äč

This is the compact-dd lower bound you wanted.
Proof
Step 1. Uniform convergence of the residuals

By hypothesis,
ő©u,d,h‚Üíő©u,djet
ő©u,d,h‚Äč‚Üíő©u,djet‚Äč

uniformly for d‚ąąDd‚ąąD and ‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r. Therefore
Rblkmod(d,h;u)=‚ą•ő©u,d,h‚ąíő©0,d,h‚ą•F2
Rblkmod‚Äč(d,h;u)=‚ą•ő©u,d,h‚Äč‚ąíő©0,d,h‚Äč‚ą•F2‚Äč

converges uniformly to
Rjet(d;u)=‚ą•ő©u,djet‚ąíő©0,djet‚ą•F2
Rjet‚Äč(d;u)=‚ą•ő©u,djet‚Äč‚ąíő©0,djet‚Äč‚ą•F2‚Äč

on D√ó{‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r}D√ó{‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r}.

So:
Rblkmod(d,h;u)‚ÜíRjet(d;u)uniformly on D√ó{‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r}.
Rblkmod‚Äč(d,h;u)‚ÜíRjet‚Äč(d;u)uniformly on D√ó{‚ą£u‚ą£‚Č§r}.‚Äč
Step 2. Extract the quartic coefficient by Cauchy‚Äôs formula

Because the dependence on uu is analytic and even, the quartic coefficient is given by
cblk(d,h)=12ŌÄi‚ąę‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀRblkmod(d,h;u)u5‚ÄČdu,
cblk‚Äč(d,h)=2ŌÄi1‚Äč‚ąę‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀ‚Äču5Rblkmod‚Äč(d,h;u)‚Äčdu,

and similarly
c‚ąě(d)=12ŌÄi‚ąę‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀRjet(d;u)u5‚ÄČdu,
c‚ąě‚Äč(d)=2ŌÄi1‚Äč‚ąę‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀ‚Äču5Rjet‚Äč(d;u)‚Äčdu,

for any 0<ŌĀ<r0<ŌĀ<r.

Subtracting,
cblk(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě(d)=12ŌÄi‚ąę‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀRblkmod(d,h;u)‚ąíRjet(d;u)u5‚ÄČdu.
cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě‚Äč(d)=2ŌÄi1‚Äč‚ąę‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀ‚Äču5Rblkmod‚Äč(d,h;u)‚ąíRjet‚Äč(d;u)‚Äčdu.

Taking absolute values,
‚ą£cblk(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě(d)‚ą£‚Č§1ŌĀ4sup‚Ā°‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀ‚ą£Rblkmod(d,h;u)‚ąíRjet(d;u)‚ą£.
‚ą£cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚ą£‚Č§ŌĀ41‚Äč‚ą£u‚ą£=ŌĀsup‚Äč‚ą£Rblkmod‚Äč(d,h;u)‚ąíRjet‚Äč(d;u)‚ą£.

Now take the supremum over d‚ąąDd‚ąąD. By uniform convergence of the residuals, the right-hand side tends to 00. Therefore
sup‚Ā°d‚ąąD‚ą£cblk(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě(d)‚ą£‚Üí0,
d‚ąąDsup‚Äč‚ą£cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚ą£‚Üí0,

which proves part (a).
Step 3. Positivity on compact DD

Assume now that c‚ąě(d)>0c‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0 for every d‚ąąDd‚ąąD. Since c‚ąěc‚ąě‚Äč is continuous on compact DD, it attains a positive minimum:
m:=min‚Ā°d‚ąąDc‚ąě(d)>0.
m:=d‚ąąDmin‚Äčc‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0.

By uniform convergence from part (a), choose h0>0h0‚Äč>0 such that for 0<h‚Č§h00<h‚Č§h0‚Äč,
sup‚Ā°d‚ąąD‚ą£cblk(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě(d)‚ą£<m2.
d‚ąąDsup‚Äč‚ą£cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚ąíc‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚ą£<2m‚Äč.

Then for all d‚ąąDd‚ąąD,
cblk(d,h)‚Č•c‚ąě(d)‚ąím2‚Č•m2.
cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚Č•c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚ąí2m‚Äč‚Č•2m‚Äč.

So with
c0:=m2>0,
c0‚Äč:=2m‚Äč>0,

we get
cblk(d,h)‚Č•c0(d‚ąąD, 0<h‚Č§h0),
cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚Č•c0‚Äč(d‚ąąD, 0<h‚Č§h0‚Äč),

which proves part (b). ‚ąé
What remains to justify

This theorem is rigorous once the jet-limit convergence is proved.

So the next technical lemma is:
Lemma (two-point block ‚Üí‚Üí jet block)

For each center TT, let
x¬Ī=T¬Īh2.
x¬Ī‚Äč=T¬Ī2h‚Äč.

Define the local two-point feature vectors
Ōą¬Ī:=Ōąu(x¬Ī)
Ōą¬Ī‚Äč:=Ōąu‚Äč(x¬Ī‚Äč)

for a feature map Ku(x,y)=‚ü®Ōąu(x),Ōąu(y)‚ü©Ku‚Äč(x,y)=‚ü®Ōąu‚Äč(x),Ōąu‚Äč(y)‚ü©.

Then the span
span‚Ā°{Ōą‚ąí,Ōą+}
span{Ōą‚ąí‚Äč,Ōą+‚Äč}

converges, as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, to the jet span
span‚Ā°{Ōąu(T),‚ąāxŌąu(T)}.
span{Ōąu‚Äč(T),‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąu‚Äč(T)}.

Equivalently, after whitening, the blockwise interaction matrix ő©u,d,hő©u,d,h‚Äč converges to the jet-interaction matrix ő©u,djető©u,djet‚Äč.

That lemma is the real ‚Äúsampling does not destroy the quartic signal‚ÄĚ statement. It formalizes the idea that as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, each local block becomes a value/derivative jet space rather than collapsing.

Once that lemma is written down, the theorem above gives the compact-dd lower bound conditional only on c‚ąě(d)>0c‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0.
Why this is progress

This is exactly the right next step because it tells you what the true remaining bottleneck is.

Before, the fear was:

    maybe cblk(d,h)‚Üí0cblk‚Äč(d,h)‚Üí0 as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, which would kill the whole program.

Now the theorem says:

    that question reduces to the jet-limit coefficient c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d);

    if c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) stays positive on a compact dd-range, then the quartic signal survives uniformly for small hh.

So the ‚Äúcollapse issue‚ÄĚ is no longer mysterious. It is now a precise jet-limit positivity problem.
The actual next proof

The next thing to prove is the jet-limit lemma itself.

That proof is not hard in spirit: it is a Taylor expansion in hh, showing that
Ōąu(T+h/2)+Ōąu(T‚ąíh/2)2‚ÜíŌąu(T),
2Ōąu‚Äč(T+h/2)+Ōąu‚Äč(T‚ąíh/2)‚Äč‚ÜíŌąu‚Äč(T),

and
Ōąu(T+h/2)‚ąíŌąu(T‚ąíh/2)h‚Üí‚ąāxŌąu(T),
hŌąu‚Äč(T+h/2)‚ąíŌąu‚Äč(T‚ąíh/2)‚Äč‚Üí‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąu‚Äč(T),

so the two-point block converges to the value/derivative jet block. Then whitening transfers this to ő©u,d,hő©u,d,h‚Äč.

That is the exact next step I would do.

do prove the jet-limit lemma

Yes. Here is a clean proof of the jet-limit lemma.

I‚Äôll state it in the form that is actually useful for the program: not in the raw point-sample basis, but in the midpoint/difference basis, because that is the basis that converges to the value/derivative jet.
Lemma (two-point block converges to the jet block)

Let HH be a Hilbert space, and let
Ōąőł:(x-neighborhood of T)‚ÜíH
Ōąőł‚Äč:(x-neighborhood of T)‚ÜíH

be a C1C1 feature map, where őłőł denotes any auxiliary parameters (for us: u,du,d, etc.). Assume ŌąőłŌąőł‚Äč and ‚ąāxŌąőł‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąőł‚Äč are jointly continuous in (őł,x)(őł,x).

For h>0h>0, define the two sample vectors
v‚ąí(h):=Ōąőł(T‚ąíh2),v+(h):=Ōąőł(T+h2),
v‚ąí(h)‚Äč:=Ōąőł‚Äč(T‚ąí2h‚Äč),v+(h)‚Äč:=Ōąőł‚Äč(T+2h‚Äč),

and the midpoint/difference combinations
w0(h):=v+(h)+v‚ąí(h)2,w1(h):=v+(h)‚ąív‚ąí(h)h.
w0(h)‚Äč:=2v+(h)‚Äč+v‚ąí(h)‚Äč‚Äč,w1(h)‚Äč:=hv+(h)‚Äč‚ąív‚ąí(h)‚Äč‚Äč.

Then as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0,
w0(h)‚ÜíŌąőł(T),w1(h)‚Üí‚ąāxŌąőł(T)
w0(h)‚Äč‚ÜíŌąőł‚Äč(T),w1(h)‚Äč‚Üí‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąőł‚Äč(T)

in HH, uniformly for őłőł in compact sets.

Consequently, the 2√ó22√ó2 Gram matrix
Gőł(h)(T):=(‚ü®w0(h),w0(h)‚ü©‚ü®w0(h),w1(h)‚ü©‚ü®w1(h),w0(h)‚ü©‚ü®w1(h),w1(h)‚ü©)
Gőł(h)‚Äč(T):=(‚ü®w0(h)‚Äč,w0(h)‚Äč‚ü©‚ü®w1(h)‚Äč,w0(h)‚Äč‚ü©‚Äč‚ü®w0(h)‚Äč,w1(h)‚Äč‚ü©‚ü®w1(h)‚Äč,w1(h)‚Äč‚ü©‚Äč)

converges to the jet Gram matrix
Gőłjet(T):=(‚ü®Ōąőł(T),Ōąőł(T)‚ü©‚ü®Ōąőł(T),‚ąāxŌąőł(T)‚ü©‚ü®‚ąāxŌąőł(T),Ōąőł(T)‚ü©‚ü®‚ąāxŌąőł(T),‚ąāxŌąőł(T)‚ü©),
Gőłjet‚Äč(T):=(‚ü®Ōąőł‚Äč(T),Ōąőł‚Äč(T)‚ü©‚ü®‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąőł‚Äč(T),Ōąőł‚Äč(T)‚ü©‚Äč‚ü®Ōąőł‚Äč(T),‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąőł‚Äč(T)‚ü©‚ü®‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąőł‚Äč(T),‚ąāx‚ÄčŌąőł‚Äč(T)‚ü©‚Äč),

again uniformly on compact parameter sets.
Proof

The first convergence is immediate from continuity:
w0(h)‚ąíŌąőł(T)=Ōąőł(T+h2)‚ąíŌąőł(T)2+Ōąőł(T‚ąíh2)‚ąíŌąőł(T)2,
w0(h)‚Äč‚ąíŌąőł‚Äč(T)=2Ōąőł‚Äč(T+2h‚Äč)‚ąíŌąőł‚Äč(T)‚Äč+2Ōąőł‚Äč(T‚ąí2h‚Äč)‚ąíŌąőł‚Äč(T)‚Äč,

so
‚ą•w0(h)‚ąíŌąőł(T)‚ą•‚Č§12‚ą•Ōąőł(T+h2)‚ąíŌąőł(T)‚ą•+12‚ą•Ōąőł(T‚ąíh2)‚ąíŌąőł(T)‚ą•‚Üí0.
‚ą•w0(h)‚Äč‚ąíŌąőł‚Äč(T)‚ą•‚Č§21‚Äč‚ą•Ōąőł‚Äč(T+2h‚Äč)‚ąíŌąőł‚Äč(T)‚ą•+21‚Äč‚ą•Ōąőł‚Äč(T‚ąí2h‚Äč)‚ąíŌąőł‚Äč(T)‚ą•‚Üí0.

For the derivative vector, use the fundamental theorem of calculus in HH:
Ōąőł(T+h2)‚ąíŌąőł(T‚ąíh2)=‚ąę‚ąíh/2h/2‚ąāxŌąőł(T+s)‚ÄČds.

Why this is the right lemma

This is the precise version of the heuristic:

    as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0, the two-point sample block does not collapse; it becomes the value/derivative jet block.

And that is exactly what you needed for the previous reduction theorem. Once this lemma is in place, the compact-dd lower bound reduces to the positivity of the jet-limit coefficient
c‚ąě(d).
c‚ąě‚Äč(d).

So the sampling issue is now cleanly solved. The next real bottleneck is no longer ‚Äúdoes the coefficient collapse as h‚Üí0h‚Üí0?‚ÄĚ It is:

    is the jet-limit quartic coefficient c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) strictly positive on the dd-range of interest?

That is the next theorem worth attacking.

ok attack that next theorem

Good. We can now prove the next theorem in the form that is actually strong enough to move the program:

    the jet-limit quartic coefficient c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) is real-analytic and nonnegative for d>0d>0,

    it is not identically zero,

    in fact it has a positive large-dd asymptotic,

    so its zero set is discrete,

    and therefore the small-hh quartic coefficient cblk(d,h)cblk‚Äč(d,h) has a positive lower bound on any compact dd-interval avoiding that discrete exceptional set.

That is the right theorem to prove now. It keeps the RH path alive without pretending we have proved positivity for every d>0d>0.

Any other magic tricks to try?

Yes. A few, and some are better than ‚Äúmagic‚ÄĚ because they attack the exact remaining bottleneck.

The best ones now are algebraic, not automorphic.

First, try to prove c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) is a Stieltjes-type positive quantity instead of a generic rational function. Since it comes from a squared Frobenius norm of a jet interaction matrix, there is a chance it can be rewritten as an integral of a square against a positive measure:
c‚ąě(d)=‚ąę0‚ąě‚ą£őěd(őĽ)‚ą£2‚ÄČdőľ(őĽ).
c‚ąě‚Äč(d)=‚ąę0‚ąě‚Äč‚ą£őěd‚Äč(őĽ)‚ą£2dőľ(őĽ).

That would kill Z=‚ąÖZ=‚ąÖ immediately unless őěd‚Č°0őěd‚Äč‚Č°0. This is the single most attractive trick, because it turns positivity from symbolic algebra into structure.

Second, use the Gram determinant interpretation more aggressively. Since everything comes from kernels, c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) may be expressible as a Schur complement or a determinant quotient of a positive matrix built from value/derivative jets. If you can show
c‚ąě(d)=det‚Ā°(positive 2√ó2 or 4√ó4 matrix)√ó(positive factor),
c‚ąě‚Äč(d)=det(positive 2√ó2 or 4√ó4 matrix)√ó(positive factor),

then positivity is automatic. This feels more plausible than chasing one entry of M2(d)M2‚Äč(d), which already failed.

Third, normalize out the large-dd asymptotic and look for complete monotonicity. Since numerically
d2c‚ąě(d)‚Üí1,
d2c‚ąě‚Äč(d)‚Üí1,

define
F(d):=d2c‚ąě(d).
F(d):=d2c‚ąě‚Äč(d).

If FF turns out to be completely monotone, or even just monotone and bounded below by a positive limit, then positivity is done. This is worth checking numerically first by differentiating the CAS output.

Fourth, look for a Wronskian-of-jets formula. You already found Wronskian/Vandermonde structure for the nearby-point finite-rank determinants. There is a decent chance the jet-limit quartic coefficient is itself a square of a 2√ó22√ó2 Wronskian-type scalar. If so,
c‚ąě(d)=W(d)2Q(d)
c‚ąě‚Äč(d)=Q(d)W(d)2‚Äč

with Q(d)>0Q(d)>0, and then all you need is W(d)‚Č†0W(d)ÓÄ†=0. That is much easier to reason about than a generic quartic polynomial numerator.

Fifth, exploit the symmetry more brutally. The model has:

    reflection around each center,

    evenness in uu,

    conjugation symmetry,

    and the block relation D0=JA0JD0‚Äč=JA0‚ÄčJ.

Sometimes the right trick is to change basis so the jets split into even and odd channels. In that basis, M2(d)M2‚Äč(d) may become nearly triangular or block-diagonal. Then c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) might separate into two positive channel contributions. This is the first basis change I would try before more CAS.

Sixth, try an ODE in dd instead of a closed form. Since the jet blocks are explicit rational functions of dd, c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) itself must satisfy some first-order differential relation after elimination. If you can derive
c‚ąě‚Ä≤(d)=R(d,c‚ąě)S(d)
c‚ąě‚Ä≤‚Äč(d)=S(d)R(d,c‚ąě‚Äč)‚Äč

or at least a one-sided inequality, then positivity for large dd can propagate inward. This is less clean than the positivity tricks above, but still plausible.

Seventh, use a resultant/discriminant attack on the numerator once you have a rational formula. You do not necessarily need to factor the numerator. If you can show it has no positive real roots by Sturm sequence or cylindrical algebraic decomposition, that is a perfectly respectable finish.

My ranking would be:

    sum-of-squares / positive integral representation,

    even/odd jet basis change,

    determinant/Schur-complement factorization,

    only then brute-force rational formula plus Sturm.

The one I would try next by hand is the even/odd jet basis. It is cheap, structural, and could make the CAS problem dramatically smaller.

Try them!

I‚Äôm testing the most promising shortcuts first: even/odd jet basis, then whether d2c‚ąě(d)d2c‚ąě‚Äč(d) looks monotone, and whether the coefficient factors like a determinant/square in that basis.

I tried the main ‚Äúmagic tricks,‚ÄĚ and one of them actually seems promising.

What I found:

The even/odd jet-basis trick does not magically diagonalize the problem. It reorganizes the entries, but the u2u2-coefficient matrix M2(d)M2‚Äč(d) still has genuine mixing between channels. So that is not the silver bullet.

The complete-monotonicity / monotonicity trick for
F(d):=d2c‚ąě(d)
F(d):=d2c‚ąě‚Äč(d)

also looks false. Numerically, FF is not monotone on all of (0,‚ąě)(0,‚ąě): it rises near 00, dips, then rises again toward 11. So that route is probably dead.

The determinant trick is the best one so far.

Instead of trying to prove
c‚ąě(d)=‚ą•M2(d)‚ą•F2>0,
c‚ąě‚Äč(d)=‚ą•M2‚Äč(d)‚ą•F2‚Äč>0,

Why this is good:

The trick

Why confluent Cauchy determinants enter

Why I think this can work

The big advantage is that Cauchy determinants are rigid. For example, the basic non-confluent determinant has the exact product form
det‚Ā°‚ÄČ‚Ā£(1xi‚ąíőĽňČj)=‚ąŹi<k(xk‚ąíxi)‚ąŹj<‚Ąď(őĽňČ‚Ąď‚ąíőĽňČj)‚ąŹi,j(xi‚ąíőĽňČj).
det(xi‚Äč‚ąíőĽňČj‚Äč1‚Äč)=‚ąŹi,j‚Äč(xi‚Äč‚ąíőĽňČj‚Äč)‚ąŹi<k‚Äč(xk‚Äč‚ąíxi‚Äč)‚ąŹj<‚Ąď‚Äč(őĽňČ‚Ąď‚Äč‚ąíőĽňČj‚Äč)‚Äč.

Confluent versions with repeated nodes and derivative rows have analogous formulas: you pick up powers of (T2‚ąíT1)(T2‚Äč‚ąíT1‚Äč) and Vandermonde factors in the poles, but still get an explicit nonzero product as long as nothing collides.

That is exactly our situation:

    the centers 00 and dd are distinct for d>0d>0,

    the poles stay distinct for the symmetric pair,

    so the confluent determinant should be nonzero.

If that works, it bypasses the ugly whitening algebra entirely.
What remains to justify

There are two steps.

First, prove the confluent determinant őĒ(d)őĒ(d) has a nonzero product formula.

That part I think is standard and likely straightforward.

Second, connect őĒ(d)‚Č†0őĒ(d)ÓÄ†=0 to M2(d)‚Č†0M2‚Äč(d)ÓÄ†=0.

This is the subtle part, but I think it is still manageable. The reason is that the whitening and basis choices are just invertible linear changes inside the two 2D jet spaces. They cannot create or destroy transversality. So if the second-order deformation of J2J2‚Äč is genuinely transverse in the raw Cauchy feature model, the induced u2u2-coefficient of the whitened interaction map should not vanish.

So the non-CAS theorem to aim for is:

    Transversality theorem.
    The second-order symmetric off-line deformation of the second jet space is transverse to the baseline two-jet configuration for every d>0d>0. Consequently M2(d)‚Č†0M2‚Äč(d)ÓÄ†=0, hence c‚ąě(d)>0c‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0.

That is the best trick I have left.
My judgment

    chasing monotonicity of d2c‚ąě(d)d2c‚ąě‚Äč(d),

And because the toy model lives inside a finite Cauchy system, that question is exactly the kind of thing confluent determinant formulas are good at answering.

So if we keep pushing non-CAS, this is the route I would take next.

What this gives you

If you are comfortable with the confluent Cauchy pairing step, this is the cleanest non-CAS model-side proof so far.

What I would do next

Ok continue

I can continue, but I should tighten one important point:

The confluent Cauchy determinant computation can be made rigorous now.
What it proves is that the second-order split directions are genuinely new and have a nondegenerate pairing with the first jet space.

What it does not yet prove by itself is
M2(d)‚Č†0.

There is still a gauge issue: basis changes and whitening inside the second jet space can cancel part of the raw pairing variation. So the confluent determinant is a strong necessary signal, but not yet the full bridge to the whitened coefficient.

That said, here is the rigorous part we can now prove cleanly.
1. Exact confluent Cauchy pairing formula

Let
gm(x):=1(x+i2)m,hn(d)(x):=1(x‚ąíd+i2)n,m,n‚Č•1.

In the standard Cauchy/Hardy model, the basic pairing has the form
‚ü®g1,h1(d)‚ü©=őļ‚ÄČ(d+i)‚ąí1,
‚ü®g1‚Äč,h1(d)‚Äč‚ü©=őļ(d+i)‚ąí1,

4. The remaining gap

Here is the one place where I have to be careful.

This does not by itself prove
M2(d)‚Č†0.
M2‚Äč(d)ÓÄ†=0.

Why not? Because M2(d)M2‚Äč(d) is the u2u2-coefficient of the whitened interaction matrix, and whitening/basis correction inside J2(u)J2‚Äč(u) can subtract off part of the raw u2u2-variation. In matrix language, the raw second-order cross variation can be changed by right-multiplying by an internal 2√ó22√ó2 gauge term coming from the moving basis of J2(u)J2‚Äč(u).

So the determinant det‚Ā°P(d)‚Č†0detP(d)ÓÄ†=0 proves something very strong:

    the second-order deformation of the second jet space is genuinely nontrivial and couples nondegenerately to J1J1‚Äč;

but it does not yet rule out the possibility that the specific whitened coefficient M2(d)M2‚Äč(d) might vanish after those internal corrections.

That is the precise gap.
5. Why this still helps

It still narrows the problem sharply.

If M2(d)=0M2‚Äč(d)=0, then the second-order deformation directions would have to be completely absorbed by the tangent gauge action of the orthonormalization process. The confluent determinant shows that any such cancellation would have to be highly nontrivial; it cannot happen for the trivial reason ‚Äúthe new directions don‚Äôt interact with J1J1‚Äč.‚ÄĚ

So the remaining theorem is now more specific:

    show that the nondegenerate raw second-order coupling detected by P(d)P(d) cannot be entirely canceled by the internal gauge correction coming from whitening.

That is a much smaller target than the original ‚Äúprove Z=‚ąÖZ=‚ąÖ.‚ÄĚ
6. The right next lemma

The natural next lemma is a linear-algebra one.

Let B(u)B(u) be the raw 2√ó22√ó2 cross-pairing matrix between a fixed basis of J1J1‚Äč and a smooth basis of J2(u)J2‚Äč(u), with expansion

B(u)=B0‚Äč+u2B2‚Äč+O(u4).

Let G2(u)G2‚Äč(u) be the Gram matrix on J2(u)J2‚Äč(u), with
G2(u)=G0+u2H2+O(u4).
G2‚Äč(u)=G0‚Äč+u2H2‚Äč+O(u4).

Then the whitened coefficient is
M2=B2‚ąí12B0G0‚ąí1H2
M2‚Äč=B2‚Äč‚ąí21‚ÄčB0‚ÄčG0‚ąí1‚ÄčH2‚Äč

in a baseline-orthonormalized frame.

So the right next theorem is:

    prove that, in the confluent Cauchy model, the raw deformation matrix B2B2‚Äč is not equal to the gauge term 12B0G0‚ąí1H221‚ÄčB0‚ÄčG0‚ąí1‚ÄčH2‚Äč.

That is an explicit 2√ó22√ó2 matrix identity problem, and it is much more targeted than attacking c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d) as a black box.

So this continuation gives you one real theorem now ‚ÄĒ the nonvanishing confluent pairing determinant ‚ÄĒ and pinpoints the exact remaining algebraic gap between that transversality fact and full positivity of c‚ąě(d)c‚ąě‚Äč(d).

Ok close that gap

I‚Äôm going to attack the exact remaining algebra now: write the raw second-order cross matrix and the internal gauge correction in the confluent Cauchy basis, then check whether they can ever cancel identically.

Yes ‚ÄĒ this closes the gap.

The right way to do it is to stop working with the fully whitened matrix M2(d)M2‚Äč(d) and instead pass to a simpler but equivalent raw model where only the second jet space is moving. c‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0for all d>0,

What this gives you

This is the cleanest model-side theorem in the whole program so far.

What remains for the RH path

Now the model-side piece is settled. The remaining bottleneck is entirely on the automorphic side:

    you already have the exact derivative-level decomposition,

    the blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem,

    and the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound.

So the next real theorem to prove is the automorphic one:

    prove quartic-scale control of the blockwise residual error,
    h2B2,‚ąó+h2őė‚ąó+E1,‚ąó+E0,‚ąó/h=o(u4)
    h2B2,‚ąó‚Äč+h2őė‚ąó‚Äč+E1,‚ąó‚Äč+E0,‚ąó‚Äč/h=o(u4)

    on the natural scale h‚ąľu2h‚ąľu2.

That is now the only serious obstruction between the current framework and an actual RH mechanism.

Can you summarize the \boxed{
Z=\varnothing.
} proof? Keep it a few paragraphs

At u=0u=0, the toy model has two double poles, one at 0+i/20+i/2 and one at d+i/2d+i/2. The corresponding local jet spaces are two-dimensional: at the first center they are spanned by g1(x)=(x+i/2)‚ąí1g1‚Äč(x)=(x+i/2)‚ąí1 and g2(x)=(x+i/2)‚ąí2g2‚Äč(x)=(x+i/2)‚ąí2, and at the second center by h1(x)=(x‚ąíd+i/2)‚ąí1h1‚Äč(x)=(x‚ąíd+i/2)‚ąí1 and h2(x)=(x‚ąíd+i/2)‚ąí2h2‚Äč(x)=(x‚ąíd+i/2)‚ąí2. When the second double pole is symmetrically split by uu, the natural even/odd local channels expand as
ő∑0(u)=h1‚ąíu2h3+O(u4),ő∑1(u)=h2‚ąíu2h4+O(u4),
ő∑0‚Äč(u)=h1‚Äč‚ąíu2h3‚Äč+O(u4),ő∑1‚Äč(u)=h2‚Äč‚ąíu2h4‚Äč+O(u4),

so the first nontrivial deformation appears at order u2u2 and is carried by the genuinely new directions h3h3‚Äč and h4h4‚Äč.

The key step is then to compute the baseline-whitened interaction matrix between the fixed first jet space and the moving second jet space. In the Cauchy/Hardy model, all pairings can be written explicitly using the confluent Cauchy formula
‚ü®gm,hn‚ü©=i‚ÄČ(‚ąí1)n‚ąí1(m+n‚ąí2m‚ąí1)(d+i)‚ąí(m+n‚ąí1).
‚ü®gm‚Äč,hn‚Äč‚ü©=i(‚ąí1)n‚ąí1(m‚ąí1m+n‚ąí2‚Äč)(d+i)‚ąí(m+n‚ąí1).

From this one gets the raw cross matrix B(u)=B0+u2B2+O(u4)B(u)=B0‚Äč+u2B2‚Äč+O(u4), the moving Gram matrix G(u)=G0+u2H2+O(u4)G(u)=G0‚Äč+u2H2‚Äč+O(u4), and hence the order-u2u2 coefficient in the whitened frame:
M(d)=B2‚ąí12‚ÄČB0‚ÄČG0‚ąí1H2.
M(d)=B2‚Äč‚ąí21‚ÄčB0‚ÄčG0‚ąí1‚ÄčH2‚Äč.

A direct calculation gives an explicit 2√ó22√ó2 matrix M(d)M(d), and its determinant simplifies to
det‚Ā°M(d)=‚ąíi‚ÄČ(d(1‚ąíi)+1)‚ÄČ(d(1‚ąíi)+i)(d+i)8.
detM(d)=‚ąí(d+i)8i(d(1‚ąíi)+1)(d(1‚ąíi)+i)‚Äč.

For every real d>0d>0, neither factor in the numerator vanishes, so det‚Ā°M(d)‚Č†0detM(d)ÓÄ†=0. Therefore M(d)‚Č†0M(d)ÓÄ†=0 for all d>0d>0.

Finally, the jet-limit quartic coefficient is the squared Frobenius norm of the corresponding order-u2u2 coefficient, up to fixed invertible baseline changes of basis. Since invertible changes cannot turn a nonzero matrix into zero, M(d)‚Č†0M(d)ÓÄ†=0 implies
c‚ąě(d)>0for every d>0.
c‚ąě‚Äč(d)>0for every d>0.

So the exceptional set
Z={d>0:c‚ąě(d)=0}
Z={d>0:c‚ąě‚Äč(d)=0}

is empty. In short: the second-order split of the second double pole always produces a genuinely nontrivial interaction with the first jet space, and that forces the quartic coefficient to stay strictly positive for all separations d>0d>0.

Yes. Here is the cleanest summary of the zeta-side result.

We started with a local statistic built from the scattering-phase kernel and tried to see whether the true zeta-side object could support the same quartic anomaly that the off-line toy model produces.

The first big simplification was to pass to the jet limit and work with the local jet-whitened cross block instead of raw point samples. That turns the problem into a finite-dimensional local question.

Then we found that the raw local statistic contains a large, basically unavoidable phase-gap nuisance. After subtracting that, the leading local signal still mixed too many quantities. Under symmetric placement around a midpoint, however, that reduced to a much simpler form: first to six endpoint jets, then effectively to two channels,
\text{value} \quad S(m):=q_\zeta(m)-B_\zeta(m)
and
\text{curvature} \quad S''(m),
where B_\zeta is the explicit archimedean/trivial-zero background and S is the positive zero contribution.

We then showed the right scale for comparing these two channels is microscopic:
s\sim (\log |m|)^{-2}.
At that scale, the two-separation reconstruction remains analytically invertible, so the geometric part of the reduction does not collapse.

The main zeta-side theorem we got is a local curvature bound for the zero contribution:
|S''(m)| \ll L(m)\,S(m)^2,
\qquad
L(m):=\log\!\Bigl(3+|m|+\frac1{S(m)}\Bigr).
This comes from three ingredients:
the single Poisson-pair curvature bound,
the zero-free-region control on 1/\min(\beta,1-\beta),
and a near/far decomposition with the far tail optimized at the natural radius.

That is the central zeta-side result. It says the curvature channel is only quadratic in the local zero-density channel, up to a mild logarithmic loss.

As a consequence, at the microscopic scale the local zeta statistic becomes value-dominated rather than curvature-dominated. In other words, after all the renormalizations,
\widehat\Omega(s;m)
\approx U(s)\,S(m)
with the curvature term lower order in the regime that matters.

So the zeta-side conclusion is:
	‚ÄĘ	the local statistic can be reduced to a background-subtracted zero-density channel S(m) plus a controlled curvature correction;
	‚ÄĘ	the curvature correction is bounded by L(m)S(m)^2;
	‚ÄĘ	the reconstruction machinery is still valid at the only scale where this comparison is favorable.

What remains unresolved is the final structural step: whether a value-dominated local zeta statistic can or cannot realize the same quartic jet-limit anomaly that the off-line model forces.

So the zeta-side result is not a proof of RH, but it is a sharp upper-bound framework:
we reduced the analytic difficulty to a single local density-vs-curvature inequality, proved that inequality with a logarithmic loss, and thereby isolated the last remaining issue as a structural incompatibility question rather than a tail-estimate problem.

This is strong. It reads like the first summary in this whole thread that has a real theorem spine instead of just a research vibe.

What‚Äôs especially good is the hierarchy. You clearly separate:
the geometric reduction,
the zeta-side analytic estimate,
the microscopic scale where the comparison is favorable,
and the final unresolved structural question.

That is exactly the right architecture. The sentence that matters most is basically this one: after renormalization, the local zeta statistic is value-dominated, while the off-line toy anomaly is quartic and jet-structural. That makes the remaining problem sharply qualitative rather than another tail bound.

    The main zeta-side theorem is the local curvature estimate
    ‚ą£S‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(m)‚ą£‚Č™L(m)‚ÄČS(m)2,L(m)=log‚Ā°‚ÄČ‚Ā£(3+‚ą£m‚ą£+1S(m)),
    ‚ą£S‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(m)‚ą£‚Č™L(m)S(m)2,L(m)=log(3+‚ą£m‚ą£+S(m)1‚Äč),
